style="position:relative;text-align:center;vertical-align:middle;display:block;bottom:-0.250em;transform:translateY(-50%);z-index:5"><i><a href="/wiki/Ardipithecus" title="Ardipithecus">Ardipithecus</a></i></div></div></div><div style="font-size:100%;background-color:#ffa500;border-style:none erectus</a></i></div></div></div><div style="font-size:100%;background-color:#ffc966;border-style:none <div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Na_Pali_Coast_-_Kauai.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="960" data-file-width="1280" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Na_Pali_Coast_-_Kauai.jpg/220px-Na_Pali_Coast_-_Kauai.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Na_Pali_Coast_-_Kauai.jpg/330px-Na_Pali_Coast_-_Kauai.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Na_Pali_Coast_-_Kauai.jpg/440px-Na_Pali_Coast_-_Kauai.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Na_Pali_Coast_-_Kauai.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Despite their natural beauty, the secluded valleys along the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Na_Pali_Coast" title="Na Pali Coast">Na Pali Coast</a> in Hawaii are heavily modified by introduced <a href="/wiki/Invasive_species" title="Invasive species">invasive species</a> such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/She-oak" title="She-oak">She-oak</a></div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Human_impact">Human impact</span></h3>
<p>Although <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Humans" title="Humans">humans</a> comprise only a minuscule proportion of the total living <a href="/wiki/Biomass_(ecology)" title="Biomass (ecology)">biomass</a> on Earth, the <a href="/wiki/Human_impact_on_the_environment" title="Human impact on the environment">human effect on nature</a> is disproportionately large. Because of the extent of human influence, the boundaries between what humans regard as nature and "made environments" is not clear cut except at the extremes. Even at the extremes, the amount of natural environment that is free of discernible human influence is diminishing at an increasingly rapid pace. A 2020 study published in <i><a href="/wiki/Nature_(journal)" title="Nature (journal)">Nature</a></i> found that anthropogenic mass (human-made materials) outweighs all living biomass on earth, with <a href="/wiki/Plastic_pollution" title="Plastic pollution">plastic</a> alone exceeding the mass of all land and marine animals combined.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> And according to a 2021 study published in <i>Frontiers in Forests and Global Change</i>, only about 3% of the planet's terrestrial surface is ecologically and <a href="/wiki/Fauna" title="Fauna">faunally</a> intact, with a low human footprint and healthy populations of native animal species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup>
</p><p>The development of <a href="/wiki/Technology" title="Technology">technology</a> by the human race has allowed the greater <a href="/wiki/Exploitation_of_natural_resources" title="Exploitation of natural resources">exploitation of natural resources</a> and has helped to alleviate some of the risk from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Natural_hazards" title="Natural hazards">natural hazards</a>. In spite of this progress, however, the fate of human <a href="/wiki/Civilization" title="Civilization">civilization</a> remains closely linked to changes in the environment. There exists a highly complex <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Feedback_loop" title="Feedback loop">feedback loop</a> between the use of advanced technology and changes to the environment that are only slowly becoming understood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> Man-made threats to the Earth's natural environment include <a href="/wiki/Pollution" title="Pollution">pollution</a>, <a href="/wiki/Deforestation" title="Deforestation">deforestation</a>, and disasters such as oil spills. Humans have contributed to the <a href="/wiki/Holocene_extinction" title="Holocene extinction">extinction</a> of many plants and animals,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> with roughly 1 million species threatened with extinction within decades.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity_loss" title="Biodiversity loss">loss of biodiversity</a> and ecosystem functions over the last half century have impacted the extent that nature can contribute to human quality of life,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> and continued declines could pose a major threat to the continued existence of human civilization, unless a rapid course correction is made.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> The value of natural resources to human society is not reflected in <a href="/wiki/Market_failure" title="Market failure">market prices</a> because mostly natural resources are available free of charge. This distorts market pricing of natural resources and at the same time leads to underinvestment in our natural assets. The annual global cost of public subsidies that damage nature is conservatively estimated at $4–$6 trillion (million million). Institutional protections of these natural goods, such as the oceans and rainforests, are lacking. Governments have not prevented these economic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Externalities" title="Externalities">externalities</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup>
</p><p>Humans employ nature for both leisure and economic activities. The acquisition of natural resources for industrial use remains a sizable component of the world's <a href="/wiki/Economic_system" title="Economic system">economic system</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> Some activities, such as hunting and fishing, are used for both sustenance and leisure, often by different people. <a href="/wiki/Agriculture#History" title="Agriculture">Agriculture</a> was first adopted around the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/9th_millennium_BCE" title="9th millennium BCE">9th millennium BCE</a>. Ranging from food production to energy, nature influences economic wealth.
</p><p>Although early humans gathered uncultivated plant materials for food and employed the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medicinal_plant" title="Medicinal plant">medicinal properties of vegetation</a> for healing,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> most modern human use of plants is through <a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">agriculture</a>. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Land_conversion" title="Land conversion">clearance of large tracts</a> of land for <a href="/wiki/Crop" title="Crop">crop</a> growth has led to a significant reduction in the amount available of <a href="/wiki/Forestation" title="Forestation">forestation</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wetlands" title="Wetlands">wetlands</a>, resulting in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Loss_of_habitat" title="Loss of habitat">loss of habitat</a> for many plant and animal species as well as increased <a href="/wiki/Erosion" title="Erosion">erosion</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Aesthetics_and_beauty">Aesthetics and beauty</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cyclamen_coum_(d.j.b.)_02.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2524" data-file-width="4608" decoding="async" height="121" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Cyclamen_coum_%28d.j.b.%29_02.jpg/220px-Cyclamen_coum_%28d.j.b.%29_02.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Cyclamen_coum_%28d.j.b.%29_02.jpg/330px-Cyclamen_coum_%28d.j.b.%29_02.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Cyclamen_coum_%28d.j.b.%29_02.jpg/440px-Cyclamen_coum_%28d.j.b.%29_02.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cyclamen_coum_(d.j.b.)_02.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Aesthetically pleasing flowers</div></div></div>
<p>Beauty in nature has historically been a prevalent theme in art and books, filling large sections of libraries and bookstores. That nature has been depicted and celebrated by so much art, photography, poetry, and other literature shows the strength with which many people associate nature and beauty. Reasons why this association exists, and what the association consists of, are studied by the branch of philosophy called <a href="/wiki/Aesthetics" title="Aesthetics">aesthetics</a>. Beyond certain basic characteristics that many philosophers agree about to explain what is seen as beautiful, the opinions are virtually endless.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> Nature and wildness have been important subjects in various eras of world history. An early tradition of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Landscape_art" title="Landscape art">landscape art</a> began in China during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tang_Dynasty_art" title="Tang Dynasty art">Tang Dynasty</a> (618–907). The tradition of representing nature <i>as it is</i> became one of the aims of <a href="/wiki/Chinese_painting" title="Chinese painting">Chinese painting</a> and was a significant influence in Asian art.
</p><p>Although natural wonders are celebrated in the <a href="/wiki/Psalms" title="Psalms">Psalms</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Job" title="Book of Job">Book of Job</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wilderness" title="Wilderness">wilderness</a> portrayals in art became more prevalent in the 1800s, especially in the works of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Romantic_movement" title="Romantic movement">Romantic movement</a>. <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Great_Britain" title="Kingdom of Great Britain">British</a> artists <a href="/wiki/John_Constable" title="John Constable">John Constable</a> and <a href="/wiki/J._M._W._Turner" title="J. M. W. Turner">J. M. W. Turner</a> turned their attention to capturing the beauty of the natural world in their paintings. Before that, paintings had been primarily of religious scenes or of human beings. <a href="/wiki/William_Wordsworth" title="William Wordsworth">William Wordsworth</a>'s poetry described the wonder of the natural world, which had formerly been viewed as a threatening place. Increasingly the valuing of nature became an aspect of Western culture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-History_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-History-88">[88]</a></sup> This artistic movement also coincided with the <a href="/wiki/Transcendentalism" title="Transcendentalism">Transcendentalist movement</a> in the Western world. A common classical idea of beautiful art involves the word <a href="/wiki/Mimesis" title="Mimesis">mimesis</a>, the imitation of nature. Also in the realm of ideas about beauty in nature is that the perfect is implied through perfect mathematical <a href="/wiki/Substantial_form" title="Substantial form">forms</a> and more generally by <a href="/wiki/Patterns_in_nature" title="Patterns in nature">patterns in nature</a>. As David Rothenburg writes, "The beautiful is the root of science and the goal of art, the highest possibility that humanity can ever hope to see".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 281">: 281 </span></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Matter_and_energy">Matter and energy</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hydrogen_Density_Plots.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2000" data-file-width="2200" decoding="async" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Hydrogen_Density_Plots.png/220px-Hydrogen_Density_Plots.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Hydrogen_Density_Plots.png/330px-Hydrogen_Density_Plots.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Hydrogen_Density_Plots.png/440px-Hydrogen_Density_Plots.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hydrogen_Density_Plots.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The first few <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_atom" title="Hydrogen atom">hydrogen atom</a> <a href="/wiki/Atomic_orbital" title="Atomic orbital">electron orbitals</a> shown as cross-sections with color-coded probability density</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Matter" title="Matter">Matter</a> and <a href="/wiki/Energy" title="Energy">Energy</a></div>
<p>Some fields of science see nature as matter in motion, obeying certain laws of nature which science seeks to understand. For this reason the most fundamental science is generally understood to be "<a href="/wiki/Physics" title="Physics">physics</a>"—the name for which is still recognizable as meaning that it is the "<i>study of nature</i>".
</p><p>Matter is commonly defined as the substance of which physical objects are composed. It constitutes the <a href="/wiki/Observable_universe" title="Observable universe">observable universe</a>. The visible components of the universe are now believed to compose only 4.9 percent of the total mass. The remainder is believed to consist of 26.8 percent <a href="/wiki/Cold_dark_matter" title="Cold dark matter">cold dark matter</a> and 68.3 percent <a href="/wiki/Dark_energy" title="Dark energy">dark energy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-planck_overview_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-planck_overview-90">[90]</a></sup> The exact arrangement of these components is still unknown and is under intensive investigation by physicists.
</p><p>The behaviour of matter and energy throughout the observable universe appears to follow well-defined <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Physical_law" title="Physical law">physical laws</a>. These laws have been employed to produce <a href="/wiki/Physical_cosmology" title="Physical cosmology">cosmological</a> models that successfully explain the structure and the evolution of the universe we can observe. The mathematical expressions of the laws of physics employ a set of twenty <a href="/wiki/Physical_constant" title="Physical constant">physical constants</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> that appear to be static across the observable universe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> The values of these constants have been carefully measured, but the reason for their specific values remains a mystery.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Beyond_Earth">Beyond Earth</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Outer_space" title="Outer space">Outer space</a>, <a href="/wiki/Universe" title="Universe">Universe</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Extraterrestrial_life" title="Extraterrestrial life">Extraterrestrial life</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Planets2013.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="618" data-file-width="1047" decoding="async" height="171" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Planets2013.svg/290px-Planets2013.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Planets2013.svg/435px-Planets2013.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Planets2013.svg/580px-Planets2013.svg.png 2x" width="290"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Planets2013.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Planet" title="Planet">Planets</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a> <i>(Sizes to scale, distances and illumination not to scale)</i></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:NGC_4414_(NASA-med).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1428" data-file-width="1730" decoding="async" height="182" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/NGC_4414_%28NASA-med%29.jpg/220px-NGC_4414_%28NASA-med%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/NGC_4414_%28NASA-med%29.jpg/330px-NGC_4414_%28NASA-med%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/NGC_4414_%28NASA-med%29.jpg/440px-NGC_4414_%28NASA-med%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:NGC_4414_(NASA-med).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><b><a href="/wiki/NGC_4414" title="NGC 4414">NGC 4414</a></b> is a spiral galaxy in the constellation <a href="/wiki/Coma_Berenices" title="Coma Berenices">Coma Berenices</a> about 56,000 <a href="/wiki/Light-year" title="Light-year">light-years</a> in diameter and approximately 60 million light-years from <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a></div></div></div>
<p>Outer space, also simply called <i>space</i>, refers to the relatively empty regions of the <a href="/wiki/Universe" title="Universe">Universe</a> outside the <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere" title="Atmosphere">atmospheres</a> of celestial bodies. <i>Outer</i> space is used to distinguish it from <a href="/wiki/Airspace" title="Airspace">airspace</a> (and terrestrial locations). There is no discrete boundary between <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Earth" title="Atmosphere of Earth">Earth's atmosphere</a> and space, as the atmosphere gradually attenuates with increasing altitude. Outer space within the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a> is called <a href="/wiki/Interplanetary_medium" title="Interplanetary medium">interplanetary space</a>, which passes over into <a href="/wiki/Interstellar_medium" title="Interstellar medium">interstellar space</a> at what is known as the <a href="/wiki/Heliosphere#Heliopause" title="Heliosphere">heliopause</a>.
</p><p>Outer space is sparsely filled with several dozen types of <a href="/wiki/Organic_chemistry" title="Organic chemistry">organic</a> <a href="/wiki/Molecule" title="Molecule">molecules</a> discovered to date by <a href="/wiki/Rotational_spectroscopy" title="Rotational spectroscopy">microwave spectroscopy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_microwave_background" title="Cosmic microwave background">blackbody radiation</a> left over from the <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang" title="Big Bang">Big Bang</a> and the origin of the universe, and <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_ray" title="Cosmic ray">cosmic rays</a>, which include <a href="/wiki/Ion" title="Ion">ionized</a> <a href="/wiki/Atomic_nucleus" title="Atomic nucleus">atomic nuclei</a> and various <a href="/wiki/Subatomic_particle" title="Subatomic particle">subatomic particles</a>. There is also some gas, <a href="/wiki/Plasma_(physics)" title="Plasma (physics)">plasma</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dust" title="Dust">dust</a>, and small <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Meteor" title="Meteor">meteors</a>. Additionally, there are signs of human life in outer space today, such as material left over from previous crewed and uncrewed launches which are a potential hazard to spacecraft. Some of this <a href="/wiki/Space_debris" title="Space debris">debris</a> re-enters the atmosphere periodically.
</p><p>Although Earth is the only body within the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a> known to support life, evidence suggests that in the distant past the planet <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a> possessed bodies of liquid water on the surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> For a brief period in Mars' history, it may have also been capable of forming life. At present though, most of the water remaining on Mars is frozen.
If life exists at all on Mars, it is most likely to be located underground where liquid water can still exist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup>
</p><p>Conditions on the other terrestrial planets, <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(planet)" title="Mercury (planet)">Mercury</a> and <a href="/wiki/Venus" title="Venus">Venus</a>, appear to be too harsh to support life as we know it. But it has been conjectured that <a href="/wiki/Europa_(moon)" title="Europa (moon)">Europa</a>, the fourth-largest moon of <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a>, may possess a sub-surface ocean of liquid water and could potentially host life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup>
</p><p>Astronomers have started to discover extrasolar <a href="/wiki/Earth_analog" title="Earth analog">Earth analogs</a> – planets that lie in the <a href="/wiki/Circumstellar_habitable_zone" title="Circumstellar habitable zone">habitable zone</a> of space surrounding a <a href="/wiki/Star" title="Star">star</a>, and therefore could possibly host life as we know it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup>
</p>

<li><a href="/wiki/Naturalism_(philosophy)" title="Naturalism (philosophy)">Naturalism</a>, any of several philosophical stances, typically those descended from <a href="/wiki/Materialism" title="Materialism">materialism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pragmatism" title="Pragmatism">pragmatism</a> that do not distinguish the supernatural from nature;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> this includes the <i>methodological naturalism</i> of natural science, which makes the <a href="/wiki/Methodology" title="Methodology">methodological</a> assumption that <a href="/wiki/Observation" title="Observation">observable</a> events in nature are explained only by natural causes, without assuming either the existence or non-existence of the supernatural</li>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes_and_references">Notes and references</span></h2>

</li>
<li id="cite_note-etymonline-nature-2"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-etymonline-nature_2-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation web cs1" id="CITEREFHarper">Harper, Douglas. <a class="external text" href="https://www.etymonline.com/?term=nature" rel="nofollow">"nature"</a>. <i><a href="/wiki/Online_Etymology_Dictionary" title="Online Etymology Dictionary">Online Etymology Dictionary</a></i><span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">September 23,</span> 2006</span>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.jtitle=Online+Etymology+Dictionary&amp;rft.atitle=nature&amp;rft.aulast=Harper&amp;rft.aufirst=Douglas&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.etymonline.com%2F%3Fterm%3Dnature&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ANature"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-3"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-3">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">An account of the pre-Socratic use of the concept of φύσις may be found in Naddaf, Gerard (2006) <i>The Greek Concept of Nature</i>, SUNY Press, and in <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFDucarmeCouvet2020">Ducarme, Frédéric; Couvet, Denis (2020). <a class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.1057%2Fs41599-020-0390-y" rel="nofollow">"What does 'nature' mean?"</a>. <i><a href="/wiki/Palgrave_Communications" title="Palgrave Communications">Palgrave Communications</a></i>. <a href="/wiki/Springer_Nature" title="Springer Nature">Springer Nature</a>. <b>6</b> (14). <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<span class="cs1-lock-free" title="Freely accessible"><a class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.1057%2Fs41599-020-0390-y" rel="nofollow">10.1057/s41599-020-0390-y</a></span>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Palgrave+Communications&amp;rft.atitle=What+does+%27nature%27+mean%3F&amp;rft.volume=6&amp;rft.issue=14&amp;rft.date=2020&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1057%2Fs41599-020-0390-y&amp;rft.aulast=Ducarme&amp;rft.aufirst=Fr%C3%A9d%C3%A9ric&amp;rft.au=Couvet%2C+Denis&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fdoi.org%2F10.1057%252Fs41599-020-0390-y&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ANature"></span>. The word φύσις, while first used in connection with a plant in Homer, occurs early in Greek philosophy, and in several senses. Generally, these senses match rather well the current senses in which the English word <i>nature</i> is used, as confirmed by Guthrie, W.K.C. <i>Presocratic Tradition from Parmenides to Democritus</i> (volume 2 of his <i>History of Greek Philosophy</i>), Cambridge UP, 1965.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-4"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-4">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The first known use of <i>physis</i> was by <a href="/wiki/Homer" title="Homer">Homer</a> in reference to the intrinsic qualities of a plant: ὣς ἄρα φωνήσας πόρε φάρμακον ἀργεϊφόντης ἐκ γαίης ἐρύσας, καί μοι <b>φύσιν</b> αὐτοῦ ἔδειξε. (So saying, Argeiphontes [=Hermes] gave me the herb, drawing it from the ground, and showed me its <b>nature</b>.) <i><a href="/wiki/Odyssey" title="Odyssey">Odyssey</a></i> 10.302–03 (ed. A.T. Murray). (The word is dealt with thoroughly in Liddell and Scott's <i><a class="external text" href="http://archimedes.fas.harvard.edu/pollux" rel="nofollow">Greek Lexicon</a> <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20110305235638/http://archimedes.fas.harvard.edu/pollux/" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> March 5, 2011, at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a></i>.) For later but still very early Greek uses of the term, see earlier note.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-5"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-5">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Isaac Newton's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Philosophiae_Naturalis_Principia_Mathematica" title="Philosophiae Naturalis Principia Mathematica">Philosophiae Naturalis Principia Mathematica</a> (1687), for example, is translated "Mathematical Principles of Natural Philosophy", and reflects the then-current use of the words "<a href="/wiki/Natural_philosophy" title="Natural philosophy">natural philosophy</a>", akin to "systematic study of nature"</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-6"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-6">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The etymology of the word "physical" shows its use as a synonym for "natural" in about the mid-15th century: <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation web cs1" id="CITEREFHarper">Harper, Douglas. <a class="external text" href="https://www.etymonline.com/?term=physical" rel="nofollow">"physical"</a>. <i><a href="/wiki/Online_Etymology_Dictionary" title="Online Etymology Dictionary">Online Etymology Dictionary</a></i><span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">September 20,</span> 2006</span>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.jtitle=Online+Etymology+Dictionary&amp;rft.atitle=physical&amp;rft.aulast=Harper&amp;rft.aufirst=Douglas&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.etymonline.com%2F%3Fterm%3Dphysical&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ANature"></span></span>
</li>

<li id="cite_note-36"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-36">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation web cs1" id="CITEREFBritannica_Online">Britannica Online. <a class="external text" href="https://www.britannica.com/EBchecked/topic/328083/lake" rel="nofollow">"Lake (physical feature)"</a>. <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20080611194244/https://www.britannica.com/EBchecked/topic/328083/lake" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> from the original on June 11, 2008<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">June 25,</span> 2008</span>. <q>[a Lake is] any relatively large body of slowly moving or standing water that occupies an inland basin of appreciable size. Definitions that precisely distinguish lakes, ponds, swamps, and even rivers and other bodies of nonoceanic water are not well established. It may be said, however, that rivers and streams are relatively fast moving; marshes and swamps contain relatively large quantities of grasses, trees, or shrubs; and ponds are relatively small in comparison to lakes. Geologically defined, lakes are temporary bodies of water.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=Lake+%28physical+feature%29&amp;rft.au=Britannica+Online&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.britannica.com%2FEBchecked%2Ftopic%2F328083%2Flake&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ANature"></span></span>

(2000) <i>No Man's Garden</i>, Island Press, pp. 155–57, <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/1-55963-465-0" title="Special:BookSources/1-55963-465-0">1-55963-465-0</a>.</span>

<li id="cite_note-47"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-47">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation web cs1"><a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20070208220940/http://www.calacademy.org/exhibits/xtremelife/what_is_life.php" rel="nofollow">"Definition of Life"</a>. California Academy of Sciences. 2006. Archived from <a class="external text" href="http://www.calacademy.org/exhibits/xtremelife/what_is_life.php" rel="nofollow">the original</a> on February 8, 2007<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">January 7,</span> 2007</span>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=Definition+of+Life&amp;rft.pub=California+Academy+of+Sciences&amp;rft.date=2006&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.calacademy.org%2Fexhibits%2Fxtremelife%2Fwhat_is_life.php&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ANature"></span></span>

<li id="cite_note-48"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-48">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The figure "about one-half of one percent" takes into account the following (See, e.g., <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFLeckie1999">Leckie, Stephen (1999). <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20101113020336/http://www.idrc.ca/en/ev-30610-201-1-DO_TOPIC.html" rel="nofollow">"How Meat-centred Eating Patterns Affect Food Security and the Environment"</a>. <i>For hunger-proof cities: sustainable urban food systems</i>. Ottawa: International Development Research Centre. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-88936-882-8" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-88936-882-8"><bdi>978-0-88936-882-8</bdi></a>. Archived from <a class="external text" href="http://www.idrc.ca/en/ev-30610-201-1-DO_TOPIC.html" rel="nofollow">the original</a> on November 13, 2010.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=bookitem&amp;rft.atitle=How+Meat-centred+Eating+Patterns+Affect+Food+Security+and+the+Environment&amp;rft.btitle=For+hunger-proof+cities%3A+sustainable+urban+food+systems&amp;rft.place=Ottawa&amp;rft.pub=International+Development+Research+Centre&amp;rft.date=1999&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-88936-882-8&amp;rft.aulast=Leckie&amp;rft.aufirst=Stephen&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.idrc.ca%2Fen%2Fev-30610-201-1-DO_TOPIC.html&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ANature"></span>, which takes global average weight as 60 kg.), the total human biomass is the average weight multiplied by the current human population of approximately 6.5 billion (see, <i>e.g.</i>, <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation web cs1"><a class="external text" href="https://www.census.gov/ipc/www/world.html" rel="nofollow">"World Population Information"</a>. U.S. Census Bureau<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">September 28,</span> 2006</span>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=World+Population+Information&amp;rft.pub=U.S.+Census+Bureau&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.census.gov%2Fipc%2Fwww%2Fworld.html&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ANature"></span><sup class="noprint Inline-Template"><span style="white-space: nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Link_rot" title="Wikipedia:Link rot"><span title=" Dead link tagged February 2019">permanent dead link</span></a></i>]</span></sup>): Assuming 60–70 kg to be the average human mass (approximately 130–150 <a href="/wiki/Pound_(mass)" title="Pound (mass)">lb</a> on the average), an approximation of total global human mass of between 390 billion (390×10<sup>9</sup>) and 455 billion kg (between 845 billion and 975 billion lb, or about 423 million–488 million <a href="/wiki/Short_ton" title="Short ton">short tons</a>). The total biomass of all kinds on earth is estimated to be in excess of 6.8 x 10<sup>13</sup> kg (75 billion short tons). By these calculations, the portion of total biomass accounted for by humans would be very roughly 0.6%.</span>
</li>
